        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b19138"><meta property="og:title" content="Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/acsami.7b19138.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are prospective alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale energy-storage applications, owing to the abundant resources of sodium. Metal sulfides are deemed to be promising anode materials for SIBs due to their low-cost and eco-friendliness. Herein, for the first time, series of copper sulfides (Cu2S, Cu7S4, and Cu7KS4) are controllably synthesized via a facile electrochemical route in KCl–NaCl–Na2S molten salts. The as-prepared Cu2S with micron-sized flakes structure is first investigated as anode of SIBs, which delivers a capacity of 430 mAh g–1 with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.9% at a current density of 100 mA g–1. Moreover, the Cu2S anode demonstrates superior capability (337 mAh g–1 at 20 A g–1, corresponding to 50 C) and ultralong cycle performance (88.2% of capacity retention after 5000 cycles at 5 A g–1, corresponding to 0.0024% of fade rate per cycle). Meanwhile, the pseudocapacitance contribution and robust porous structure in situ formed during cycling endow the Cu2S anodes with outstanding rate capability and enhanced cyclic performance, which are revealed by kinetics analysis and ex situ characterization."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/acsami.7b19138.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931887fe5af654b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887fe5af654b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '53c48267-5d0c-43e2-b65d-2136322a6730'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://www2.snapengage.com/chatjs/ServiceGetConfig?w=921a4ec7-cecf-43a6-afa5-6cd1346520b1"></script><script charset="utf-8" src="https://pubs.acs.org/products/achs/releasedAssets/js/lazy-imports-6922fe6a4ca6b2e0cd9a.js"></script><script type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931887fe5af654b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b19138" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="931887fe5af654b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b19138", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Shijie Cheng</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">State Key Laboratory of Advanced Electromagnetic Engineering and Technology, School of Electrical and Electronic Engineering and Huazhong University of Science and Technology, Wuhan 430074, Hubei, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Shijie++Cheng" data-id="article_author_search">Shijie Cheng</a></span></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Kai Jiang</span><strong>*</strong><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Kai Jiang</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">State Key <a href="mailto:kjiang@hust.edu.cn">kjiang@hust.edu.cn</a> (K.J.).</span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Kai++Jiang" data-id="article_author_search">Kai Jiang</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0004.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are prospective alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale energy-storage applications, owing to the abundant resources of sodium. Metal sulfides are deemed to be promising anode materials for SIBs due to their low-cost and eco-friendliness. Herein, for the first time, series of copper sulfides (Cu<sub>2</sub>S, Cu<sub>7</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and Cu<sub>7</sub>KS<sub>4</sub>) are controllably synthesized via a facile electrochemical route in KCl–NaCl–Na<sub>2</sub>S molten salts. The as-prepared Cu<sub>2</sub>S with micron-sized flakes structure is first investigated as anode of SIBs, which delivers a capacity of 430 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.9% at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the Cu<sub>2</sub>S anode demonstrates superior capability (337 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 50 C) and ultralong cycle performance (88.2% of capacity retention after 5000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to 0.0024% of fade rate per cycle). Meanwhile, the pseudocapacitance contribution and robust porous structure in situ formed during cycling endow the Cu<sub>2</sub>S anodes with outstanding rate capability and enhanced cyclic performance, which are revealed by kinetics analysis and ex situ characterization.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D8016%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DHaomiao%2BLi%252C%2BKangli%2BWang%252C%2BShijie%2BCheng%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b19138%26title%3DControllable%2BElectrochemical%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BCopper%2BSulfides%2Bas%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%2Bwith%2BSuperior%2BRate%2BCapability%2Band%2BUltralong%2BCycle%2BLife%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D8025%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials"> Anode materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291338&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical properties"> Electrical properties </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291353&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Rate capability"> Rate capability </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrochemical+Synthesis&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrochemical%20synthesis">electrochemical synthesis</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Copper+Sulfides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_copper%20sulfides">copper sulfides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery+Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery%20anodes">sodium-ion battery anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High+Rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high%20rate%20capability">high rate capability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long+Cycle+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long%20cycle%20life">long cycle life</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Energy-storage technologies are deemed to be the key of integrating intermittent renewable energy generation (such as wind and solar) into the grid. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> As a promising energy-storage device, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been prompting extensive attentions in both academic community and industry, owing to their abundant reserves of Na resource and competitive power/energy density. However, compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> is 35% larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup>, which brings greater challenges to SIBs for searching suitable host materials to accommodate the larger Na<sup>+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> In the past several years, tremendous efforts have been devoted to developing cathode materials for SIBs, such as polyanionic compounds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 7 to 10">(7−10)</a> layered oxides <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11−14)</a> and Prussian-blue-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> providing numerous choices for SIBs. Nevertheless, the shortage of high-performance anode materials has been stunting the development of SIBs because Na<sup>+</sup> cannot intercalate into the commercialized graphite anode, which is commonly used in LIBs. Even though the studies on anode materials, such as hard carbon-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 18 to 22">(18−22)</a> transition-metal oxide materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> and metallic compounds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 27 to 31">(27−31)</a> have been progressive in recent years, most of those materials are still suffering from low capacity, poor cyclic performance, and limited rate capability. Thus, developing anode materials with all merits of high capacity, excellent durability, and superior rate capability is urgent, however with great challenge for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Metal sulfides, as promising anode materials, have been intensively investigated in SIBs in recent years due to the merits of low cost, eco-friendliness, and good conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Several typical metal sulfides, such as SnS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a> Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39,40)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 41 to 43">(41−43)</a> NiS, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> and FeS<sub>2</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 45 to 48">(45−48)</a> exhibit impressive Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance when serving as anode of SIBs. Thereinto, one class of metal sulfides, incorporating both conversion and alloying reactions, could provide high Na<sup>+</sup>-storage capacity, for instance, SnS<sub>2</sub> (1136.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (947.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (625.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). However, the consequent severe volume change impedes the improvement of their long-term cycle performance. For example, Qu et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> designed a layered SnS<sub>2</sub>@rGO composite via hydrothermal method and demonstrated its rate performance (544 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and cycle life (84% of capacity retention after 400 cycles). However, the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage capacity of other kinds of metal sulfides, such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> NiS, and FeS<sub>2</sub> are mainly contributed from conversion reaction. Inert metal products formed during the sodiation process are contributed to enhance the cycle stability by buffering the volume expansion and improving conductivity of electrodes. For example, Ren et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> reported an ultrathin MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets@C composite as anode of SIBs, achieving 265 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of capacity after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Pyrite FeS<sub>2</sub> microspheres, reported by Chen et al., <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> displayed excellent cycle stability (90% capacity retention for 20 000 cycles with a cutoff voltage of 0.8 V) and rate capability (170 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Copper sulfides, a type of functional semiconductor with various stoichiometrics, have wide applications in sensors, solar cells, and optoelectronic devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 51 and 52">(51,52)</a> Moreover, the merits of comparable band gap energy (<i>E</i><sub>g</sub> = 1.2 eV), excellent electronic conductivity (ca. 10<sup>4</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>), eco-friendliness, and abundant reserves also endowed copper sulfides a promising prospective in application of energy-storage batteries, such as LIBs and SIBs. Actually, typical copper sulfides (CuS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S) have been investigated in LIBs and shown impressive Li<sup>+</sup>-storage performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 53 and 54">(53,54)</a> However, the major issue hindering the application of copper sulfides in LIBs is its poor cycle stability.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, a novel method of electrolysis anodes directly synthesizing metal sulfides was developed. In this process, different phase structure copper sulfides (Cu<sub>2</sub>S, Cu<sub>7</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and Cu<sub>7</sub>KS<sub>4</sub>) were controllably electrochemically synthesized in molten salts, which is a facile and controllable method inspired by the Fray–Farthing–Chen process widely used for the preparation of metal/alloys <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 55 to 57">(55−57)</a> and functional materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58 ref59 ref60" aria-label="reference 58 to 60">(58−60)</a> The as-prepared copper sulfides were investigated as anode of SIBs and demonstrated excellent Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance. Especially, the Cu<sub>2</sub>S anodes with micron-sized flakes structure exhibit superior capability (337 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> capacity at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>, less than 20% capacity fading compared to initial capacity at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and ultralong cycle performance (88.2% of capacity retention after 5000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Electrolysis Setup and Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">An electrolysis setup (three-electrode setup for cyclic voltammetry (CV) test, two-electrode setup for electrochemical synthesis) was sealed in a stainless retort filled with argon and heated in a vertical tube furnace. The anode consisted of a small graphite cup containing Cu pellets (diameter 10 mm, weight 1.0 g), which was prepressed by Cu powder at a pressure of ∼10 MPa. A graphite rod (10 mm diameter) connected to a Mo wire served as cathode. For CV testing, a Ag/AgCl reference electrode was fabricated by sealing a Ag wire (0.5 mm diameter) and ∼5 g of mixture salts (3 wt % of AgCl in NaCl–KCl eutectic) into an end-closed mullite tube (i.d. = 4 mm). High-purity NaCl and KCl (&gt;99%, from Aladdin) were dried under vacuum at 120 °C for 12 h to remove residual water before being used as electrolytes together with Na<sub>2</sub>S. NaCl (44 g) and KCl (56 g) were taken, mixed, and transferred into an alumina crucible (i.d., 40 mm; depth, 100 mm). The cell was assembled and transferred into the stainless retort with continuous argon flow, then the assembly was initially held at 250 °C for 4 h and then heated up to the working temperature of 700 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrochemical Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Potentiostatic electrolysis was conducted at 700 °C using the two-electrode cell described above. Before adding Na<sub>2</sub>S into molten salts as S source, a pre-electrolysis process at 2.2 V was performed with a graphite rod anode and steel wire cathode to remove the impurities from molten salt. After the current of pre-electrolysis stabilized at a small value (called background current, after about 2 h), the steel wire was taken out and the predried Na<sub>2</sub>S was added into the cell. Copper sulfides were synthesized by applying predetermined voltages between Cu anode and graphite cathode in molten salts. After the electrolysis is finished, the anodes were lifted out of the molten salts and cooled in argon, washed with deionized water, and then dried at 60 °C over 12 h for further tests.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The phase structure, composition, and valence state of the samples were characterized by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). XRD patterns were recorded on a PANalytical X’Pert PRO (Cu Kα generator). XPS survey was carried out on an AXIS-ULTRA DLD-600W spectrometer (from Kratos Corp., Japan). Morphologies of the products were observed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM, FEI Nova NanoSEM 450).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical performance of the as-obtained products was characterized with 2025-type coin cells. The electrodes (diameter, ∼11 mm) to be measured were made by mixing the as-prepared materials (Cu<sub>2</sub>S) with Ketjen black and poly(acrylic acid)/carboxyl methyl cellulose (1:1) in deionized water in the ratio 7:2:1. The mass loading of the active material is 1.8–2.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The electrolyte is 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in ethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DME). Sodium foil and microporous glass fiber membrane (Whatman) were used as counter electrode and separator, respectively. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) were characterized on a PGSTAT302N potentiostat/galvanostat (AUTOLAB, the Netherlands), and galvanostatic cycling tests were performed on a LAND2001A battery tester (LANHE, China) in the voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V at room temperature.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a describes the design of the three-electrode cell for cyclic voltammetry testing with Ag/AgCl reference electrode, graphite working electrode, and counter electrode. Considering high solubility of Na<sub>2</sub>S and K<sub>2</sub>S in NaCl–KCl eutectic, NaCl–KCl–Na<sub>2</sub>S melts are selected as electrolyte as well as providing S source in this work. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b presents the CV curves of graphite electrode recorded in molten NaCl–KCl (−Na<sub>2</sub>S) at 700 °C. As can be seen, the electrochemical window of molten NaCl–KCl is greater than 3.3 V. The reduction/oxidation peaks at ca. −2.1 and 1.2 V (vs Ag/AgCl) are attributed to Na deposition/stripping and oxidation of Cl<sup>–</sup>, respectively. However, after adding Na<sub>2</sub>S into the cell, the broad peak around 0 V (vs Ag/AgCl) in molten NaCl–KCl–Na<sub>2</sub>S is deemed as the oxidation of S<sup>2–</sup>, which is consistent with the previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0005.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic of the three-electrode cell (a) and cyclic voltammograms of the graphite electrode in molten KCl–NaCl (−Na<sub>2</sub>S) at 700 °C (b). Scan rate: 50 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The processes of electrolytic synthesis were carried out in the two-electrode cell described above, under different electrolysis voltages (0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 V) sustaining 10 h. XRD patterns of the as-prepared products under the aforementioned voltages are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. Three stoichiometrics copper sulfides, Cu<sub>2</sub>S, Cu<sub>7</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, and Cu<sub>7</sub>KS<sub>4</sub>, were obtained by adjusting electrolysis voltage from 0.6 to 1.0 V. When the voltage is below 0.6 V, orthorhombic Cu<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS No. 23-0961) with trace of Cu<sub>1.97</sub>S is detected by XRD, which is helpful to enhance the conductivity of Cu<sub>2</sub>S. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> However, orthorhombic Cu<sub>7</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 22-0250) and tetragonal Cu<sub>7</sub>KS<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS No. 47-1334) are obtained when the voltage increases to 0.8 and 1.0 V, respectively. The morphologies of products obtained are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b–d. Micron-sized flakes with smooth surfaces are observed in the SEM images at voltage below 0.6 V (SEM images of product at 0.4 V shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The thickness of the flakes is several micrometers, whereas the diameter exceeds 100 μm. However, a flowerlike structure composed of 100 nm plates attached to the surface of flakes is formed when the voltage is above 0.8 V.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0008.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XRD patterns (a) and SEM images (b–d) of products obtained under electrolysis voltages of 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical state of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S obtained under 0.6 V is further characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The existence of C, Cu, O, and S is proved in the survey scan spectrum shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, and impurities of C and O should be introduced during the preparation of samples. From the high-resolution XPS image shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the peaks at 952.4 and 932.5 eV correspond to Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Cu<sup>1+</sup>, respectively, whereas the small peaks around 954.7 and 934.6 eV are assigned to Cu<sup>2+</sup>, which may demonstrate the presence of other stoichiometry Cu<i><sub>x</sub></i>S (1 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 2, such as Cu<sub>1.97</sub>S detected in the XRD patterns) in the Cu<sub>2</sub>S. The nonstoichiometric Cu<i><sub>x</sub></i>S with higher conductivity <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> mixed in Cu<sub>2</sub>S is helpful to improve the rate performance of SIBs. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, the peaks around 161.7 and 162.8 eV can be assigned to S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of S<sup>2–</sup>, respectively.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0001.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XPS survey scan image of Cu<sub>2</sub>S (a) and high-resolution XPS images of Cu 2p peak (b) and S 2p peak (c).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The micron-sized Cu<sub>2</sub>S flakes obtained below 0.6 V were first used as SIB anodes to evaluate their electrochemical performance. To understand the electrochemical process of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes, cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were recorded on the two-electrode coin cell with Na foil counter electrode, at scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, two broad reduction peaks around at 0.8 and 0.27 V and the corresponding oxidation peaks at 1.73 and 1.95 V can be observed at the first cycle. However, during the second cycle, the peaks at 0.27 and 0.81 V drift to 0.36 and 0.91 V, respectively, and the additional three peaks in the region of 1.09–1.96 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) appear. The nonrepeatability at the initial stage indicates an activation process during the first several cycles, as well as the decomposition of electrolyte and solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref53" aria-label="reference 45 and 53">(45,53)</a> Ex situ XRD of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at different charge/discharge states was carried out to further investigate the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, during the sodiation process, the intensity of Cu<sub>2</sub>S peaks gradually weakens and disappears until discharge voltage is below 0.3 V, meanwhile accompanying the weak peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S appearing at fully discharge state of 0.01 V. When the cell is fully charged to 2.5 V, a broad peak at 45.9 °C assigned to the (2 13 1) plane of Cu<sub>2</sub>S is observed. Accordingly, during the cathodic scan at CV curves, the reduction peaks at the region of 0.91–1.96 V are attributed to the series phase transformation triggered by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the orthorhombic Cu<sub>2</sub>S (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> However, the peak at 0.36 V suggests the conversion reaction of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Cu<sub>2</sub>S into Cu and Na<sub>2</sub>S (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> Those reduction and oxidation peaks show good stability in the subsequent cycles, indicating good reversibility of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes for Na storage.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>Galvanostatic charge/discharge is also conducted to evaluate the electrochemical performance of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the charge–discharge profiles are recorded on the half-cell with Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at 100 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, in which the Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes deliver 433.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of discharge capacity and 368 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of charge capacity, with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.9%. The irreversible capacity in the first cycle mainly results from the formation of SEI film and side reactions in cells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref44" aria-label="reference 41 and 44">(41,44)</a> The overlapped curves second and third, ∼390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> reversible capacity, and &gt;97% Coulombic efficiency show good stability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S. It is noteworthy that the polarization between charge/discharge curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>S are decreased dramatically, which is attributed to the refining of micron-sized Cu<sub>2</sub>S flakes during sodiation/desodiation, which will be discussed later in this article. As comparison, the Cu<sub>7</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and Cu<sub>7</sub>KS<sub>4</sub> obtained under 0.8 and 1.0 V are also investigated as SIB anode, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3 and S4</a>, which exhibit 514 and 367 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of initial capacities with Coulombic efficiencies of 89.7 and 91.2%, respectively.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0002.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>S (from 0.6 V) electrodes in the voltage range of 0–2.5 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (a); charge–discharge profiles for 1–3, 50, and 100 cycles of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes in the voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a presents the rate capability of the as-prepared Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at different current densities within 0.01–2.5 V. The Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes deliver capacities of 420, 418, 414, 389, 381, 375, 369, and 354 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when applying current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.6, 3.2, 6.4, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at a 200-fold increased current density of 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> (corresponding to 50 C), a high capacity of 337 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (80.2% of initial capacity) can be obtained, only less than 20% capacity loss compared to the capacity at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the excellent rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S micron-sized flakes as SIB anodes. After 90 cycles at different current densities, the discharge capacity could recover to 437 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is turned back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, reflecting good cyclic stability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> shows the charge–discharge profiles of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at different current densities (from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Although the difference of the charge and discharge curves at different current densities can be distinguished, the voltage plateaus are nearly overlapped when current density is below 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at a higher current density of 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the charge–discharge plateaus still could be detected, suggesting the superior rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes. To further evaluate the level of rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes, the rate performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>S is compared to other typical metal chalcogenide anode materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref41 ref44 ref45 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref41 ref44 ref45 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69" aria-label="reference 39, 41, 44, 45 and 64 to 69">(39,41,44,45,64−69)</a> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6</a>. The rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes exhibits evident advantage, especially at high current densities over 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, the rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes presents overwhelming superiority at high current densities, when taking capacity retention rate as the comparison parameter. To the best of our knowledge, the anode for SIBs with such superior rate performance has never been reported before.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/medium/am-2017-19138x_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performances of the as-prepared Cu<sub>2</sub>S as anode of SIBs. (a) Rate capability at different densities. (b) Comparison of rate performance with typical metal chalcogenide materials of SIB. (c) CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at various scan rates after 30 cycles. (d) Log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) plots for different redox peaks of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode. (e) Long-term cyclic properties at a current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/asset/images/large/am-2017-19138x_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b19138&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the superior rate capability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes, the kinetics processes were investigated using CV measurement. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c exhibits the CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes at different scan rates (0.1–1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) within the voltage window of 0–2.5 V. Several redox peaks can be observed at each curves, and the peak current increases with the increasing of the scan rate. Obviously, from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the peak current (<i>i</i>) is disproportionate to the square root of the scan rate (<i>v</i>), illustrating the electrochemical process of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes including non-Faradaic and Faradaic contribution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref64" aria-label="reference 64">(64)</a> Generally, the equation log(<i>i</i>) = <i>b</i> log(<i>v</i>) + log(<i>a</i>) is used to describe the relationship of peak current (<i>i</i>) to scan rate (<i>v</i>), where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The type of sodiation and desodiation depends on the value of <i>b</i>. When <i>b</i> = 0.5, the charge–discharge process is controlled by ion diffusion, whereas when <i>b</i> = 1, the process depends on pseudocapacitive control. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the linear fitting results of log(<i>v</i>) and log(<i>i</i>) for every peak. The <i>b</i> values of the six peaks are 0.92, 0.85, 1.12, 0.95, 0.91, and 1.05, respectively, which indicates that the electrochemical process is mainly contributed by pseudocapacitive behavior, endowing Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrodes with superior rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p">To demonstrate the long-term cyclic performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode, several cells were cycled at different current densities within 0.01–2.5 V. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, the Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode exhibits initial capacity of over 430 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A cm<sup>–2</sup> and almost no capacity fading after 100 cycles. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e displays long-term cycle curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which exhibits reversible capacities of 317 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. After 5000 cycles, over 88.2% of capacity retentions are achieved, corresponding to 0.0024% of fade rate per cycle, demonstrating the ultrahigh cycle stability of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode. Furthermore, the Coulombic efficiency maintains over 99% even in the 5000 cycles. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b19138/suppl_file/am7b19138_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a> shows the typical charge–discharge curves at different cycles. As can be seen, the charge–discharge profiles are almost overlapped before 2000 cycles and a pimping difference can be detected even at 5000 cycles, further suggesting the excellent cyclic properties of Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode. Moreover, as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, this Cu<sub>2</sub>S electrode from electrochemical synthesis exhibits superior stability for Na<sup>+</sup> storage compared to previous metal chalcogenide anode materials for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Comparison of the Cyclic Performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>S Electrodes with Previously Reported Typical Metal Chalcogenide Anode Materials for SIBs</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">wet chemical process</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">361</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">150</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char"
